And the quality of the position of the winds is that you pause the wind at the beginning, and suck it in with temperate suction, and do not prolong the position of the winds, but pause them lightly, and move them gently, and that the humors come to the place by equal advancement. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place already reddened and inflated, and that the redness of the blood appears.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and then consider in the disposition of the bodies.
Because that man who has tender flesh of rare pores, because you scarify him once and no more, so that the place is not ulcerated.
And command the wind-operator to amplify the scarification and proceed a little, and temper the suction with ease.
And if in the blood there is thickness, then you must scarify twice, because in the first scarification the way of subtilization to the blood and its liquidity is opened. But in the second, the scrutiny acquired from the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then the third scarification is made so that the last (is achieved).
And finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a little, it suffices us one scarification.
And if we want the extraction of much blood, we scarify with many scarifications; and if we knew that the blood is thick, then we must scarify with a deep scarification.
And the term of scarification in which the depth of the incision is only so much.
And if that one whose incision is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, then the places of the winds should be anointed more gently, more open, more resolved; if it is in summer, as is oil of aloes or oil of violets, or oil of double amaranth, or oil of quince; and if it is in winter, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or of saffron, and to those similar.
And if the superfluity is thick, hot, then the oil should be of amaranth, or oil of balsamita, or oil of aloes, or oil of fennel, and to those similar.
But if the wind-operator has wide pores of tender flesh, then he should be prohibited from oil, and he should have the places of the winds near his own application be washed with waters or with cold water of solastrum, or with quince water, or with water of borlaguas, and similar to those.
And he whose blood is of much humidity, washes his winds with sour wine, or with myrtle water and sumac, and similar to those.
And he whose superfluities are thick, washes his winds with old wine, or with water of sarsaparilla, which is marjoram, or of decoction of fennel, or of chamomile, and to those similar.
And the wind-operator should be guarded before bathing; he should be administered near the bath for an hour or two, and he should sleep near the wind-application.
What should be done to regulate the wind-operator before phlebotomy?
At the beginning, you should consider: if the wind-operator or the one who is to be phlebotomized is choleric and windy, and his blood is sharp and inflamed, then he should be given cooling causes, such as vinegar-soaked millet, endive with sour wine and lettuce, and secanabin, and syrup, and to those similar.
And pause their meals as much as possible, and birds' meat, and foods seasoned with vinegar, and to those similar.
And for him whose constitution is cold, he should be given to drink syrup of almiva, or syrup of alsecanabin of seeds, and sumac, and wine very fragrant between old and new; and that he should be commanded to use little drinking and eating, and pause his meals as much as possible, and eat little birds, and rabbits, and sparrows, and small birds, and doves.
And that on the day of wind-application and phlebotomy, the drink should be greater than the food.
And that some men should be given to drink in some hours,  tyriaca alpharac, or medicines of musk, that is, muscat, or alcelita before the wind-application or phlebotomy, or near it, so that the main members be comforted and the blood be subtiliated; and that they should not take that scalfatz.
But the winds that are scarifications should be winds that are paused on the liver, and on the spleen, and podagra, and on the navel, in the places of the groin, and the side of the hip, because these members do not suffer scarification upon themselves.
And it is not desired for those, but rather attraction of blood from the limb, as if you pause a wind on the nipple in the illness of blood flow from the blood of the nose; and use it so that it may be dissolved cold windiness of the limb, as if you pause on the belly and navel; because that thins the limb and scalfs it and removes the pain because it resolves the windiness.
And it is placed on the sores when they happen in their opening or boil, because by virtue of strong attraction the man opening and eradicates the boil from its place.
And similarly it is done when it is placed on the liver and spleen, when there is windiness in those enclosed places.
And these windinesses are administered voids with suction alone; and some times are administered with fire.
And some times are administered full of water  tebeza in the illnesses of the spleen.
And this is why the windpipe is filled and made larger, so it is water poured alone or water in which herbs are boiled for that purpose.
Form of windpipe administered with fire: its width of the orifice of two fingers, according to how we have formed it; and the quantity of the preon is the majority of a palm; and it is in the place of it, in the place that is in the circuit, the majority of it, the tract be small according to the quantity that enters it a needle.
It is made of  eram rog or citron, of large margins, lion,  enguals and terses so that it does not lead the member of its position over it.
And in the middle of it a transverse bar of eram or of iron, on which a candle is placed with the fire.
And already this windpipe is larger than the one we have recounted; and the few smaller than that according to the illnesses and the state of him who uses it.
And the windpipes of the infants and exhausted are others than the windpipes of the young and of large bodies.
And the quality of pausing this windpipe with fire on the member is that it extends the fire with wood decently in front of a small candle of wax, and places it on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle place of the windpipe, so that the fire is ignited around the part behind so that it does not burn the body of the patient.
And then it is placed on the member of the patient, and the finger is on the tract that we have said,  into that when we have the windpipes and do not indicate that we show the finger and that it escapes the vapor on that tract, then the windpipe is immediately dissolved.
And then keep the fire according to its manner, and repeat that if it is indicated of that.
But in a windpipe that is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar nor tract, and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and it is placed on the member alone.
And this is the form of it: And this windpipe as much as it is larger, so much is better, because it can receive a large quantity of water.
Chapter .xcvii.
Bloodletting is not administered in all members but in those members in which it is not possible to place windpipes, because of the smallness of those, such as the mouth and gums and similar to them; or in members naked of flesh, such as the finger and the nose and their similar.
And the quality of using those that have been found leeches that are in the waters of  poredura mundas.
And then they are laid the day and the night in water of  poredura mundas into that they are famolentas, and that no remains any cause in the belly of those; and then it is evacuated the body at the beginning with fleubotomia and with windpipes; and then it is rubbed the member of the patient into that it reddens; and then they are placed on it.
And as they are filled and case.
And if it is possible suction of the place with windpipe, it is more better in help.
And if not, then wash the place with vi agre and then with much water; and be rubbed and pressed.
And if the course of the blood is prolonged and the result of the leeches, then you put a cloth of it in cold water, and place it on top until the perspiration is abated.
And if the blood is multiplied, then pulverize on it vitriol trissat, or guallas and similar to them of the causes stipticas, into that the blood is abscissed; or are placed on the place the halves of favas excocicadas, and are laid into that the favas adhere to the place, and the blood is abscissed.
And now, if necessary, repetition of leeches, when it is possible to change those.
And if the leeches are prohibited from suspension, then be rubbed the place with recent blood or leave a needle in the places into that blood escapes; and then they are placed, because those when they feel some blood immediately adhere.
And when you want them to fall off, pulverize on them a little of aloen trissat, or salt, or ash, and they fall off immediately.
Thus begins the prolec of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is many times necessary in the art of medicine; and the restoration of fracture and dislocation accident in the bones.
Oh, son, know that these fools of doctors and the vulgar boast of this chapter and those who never at any time regarded the books of the Ancients nor read from them a letter.
And for this reason, it is made that this manner of the learned in our land is deprived; and you have not forgotten this at any time well of all; and I have not adopted this that I have presented, but for the length of reading the books of the Ancients and study on the understanding of those, while I extracted the science from those.
And then I devote myself to experiment and study throughout the length of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter all that my science contains and on which my best experiment has been passed; because I have shortened this for you others and delivered it from the occupation of elongation; and I have shortened it at the end of shortening; and I have shown it at the end of declaration; and I have formed in it many forms of the instruments that are administered in it, as it has been done by addition of declaration, as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
First Chapter.
Summaries and collections from the broken bones, from those whose introduction is necessary before we begin the remembrance of broken and diverse members of each one. Therefore, I say that in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the sermon; and the chapters to the understanding of which at the beginning is forced the artificers and to stay on the truth of those; so is you and those who are prompt to know or learn this noble art others than you.
And therefore I say that when you come across a fracture or dislocation or sprain or wound, then you must at the beginning apply yourself to phlebotomy or to drain that, or one of the two, if no cause prohibits, as is the weakness of virtue, or the one to whom you come across is a child, or old in decrepitude, or the time of vehement heat, or of vehement cold.
And then be content in his food of cold herbs and of birds' meats and of crabs; and forbid thick meats and to fill him with foods and of all food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of a cauterized abscess, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his first regimen to which he is accustomed.
And therefore, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient must be fed with nutritious foods of nourishment thick and heavy in which is viscosity, as are foods made of boiled wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and fish, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh birds, and thick meat and similar to those, and with this regimen is made coagulation of fracture more lightly and better.
And know that the broken bones when they are in the young and in the decrepit, it is not possible that they be continued and consolidated according to their nature by the drying of their limbs.
May, however, nature adjusts and amnesties over the broken bone from every part any cause seeming to glut in which is thickness that adheres and strengthens that within as they are at once conglutinated and tied together, until they come to the end of fortitude and firmness, as fire at the beginning, until no cause prohibits that of its works.
And for this cause it is necessary that you pause the foods of the patient, foods in which there is heaviness and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the hand; and similarly all the other limbs are diverse among themselves.
And you come with remembrance of all species of fracture, explained in their distinct chapter from the other.
Many diversity of fracture in itself. And they are diversified the species of fracture of the bone in their own; for there is made arched fracture another that happens in them stars; and some times is made fracture according to the length of the bone: and in the fractures of the satagia and stars and additions proceeding and not proceeding, as is done in the pen when it is prepared to write, and is fracture with plaque and disruption in the head, and is fracture small fissure.
And to every species is born or torment proper in the restoration of them according to what will come remembrance of them distinct in their places.
Many sign of fracture of the bone. And for this is known fracture of the bone: it is the tortuosity of it, and of that eminence and appearance of it to the touch, and of it when you compress that with your hand.
And therefore when there is not in the place apparent tortuosity nor is it felt when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
It is possible that there is tortuosity or small fissure, therefore you must not touch that with your hand and with compression of all; but pause upon it of the medicines of which will come remembrance near that moment the one that convenes to the place; and then be straight by subtle constriction.
And know that the place when it is curved and not split in two or anything else that does not happen in it, like it is sewn up all of its part, then when you begin of your time to rectification of it, you have to that it happens after pus caut.
And if it happens pus caut, then leave that for some days until the pus ceases; and then bandage that according to whichever manner that you can of lightness and of engineering.
And know that the restoration of it and the sewing of it is lighter than of the bone in which the injury happened.
And then strengthen that according to what will come the remembering of it.
But if in the bone there are injuries, then it is necessary extension of this broken limb of two parts, if it is the arm or the leg; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two ropes or with one rope and the hand.
And be the your position with which you pause the limb loc engual according to its natural figure, while that the parts are extended of the broken limb, then you perforate that you make return those additions with engineering and according to whichever manner that you can in their places.
And guard yourself according to your force that it does not happen upon the patient by your operation pain nor injury; and perforate yourself according to the your power that you apply one bone with the other its companion according to their best forms.
And cover that in that hour profitably you touch with your hand that, and if you see here any other cause, rectify that and bandage that for the quantity of your virtue.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as such as do foolish doctors that many times do happen pus caut by their operation, or do happen contraction in the limb as we have seen that by the operation of their foolish ways.
And the quality of the stricture of the limb tranquil is that which I recommend; for that you know that the broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their greatness and in the form of those.
And of those that are small as such as the arm and the hand and the foot and similar to those, then cover that the envelopment be of cloth very subtle.
But those that are gross as such as the thigh or the leg, then cover that in those be envelopment wide and hard, for the wide liament connects the great limb and strengthens that of all part with equal stricture, in this way that in it no blemish enters.
And then begin, then your evacuation of equation, pause liament convenient to that, upon the place of that fracture with three envelopment or four according to that the limb merits; and strengthen your hand a little with the liament.
And then come to that at the part most sovereign of the place of the fracture, and strengthen less that not force the your stricture with which you have strengthened the place of the fracture. And then elongate yourself with envelopment of the place of the fracture a little, and mollify the stricture little by little, until that you take from the place a great part.
And then take another bandage and envelop that other time over the broken place with envelopment, and then come to the envelopment to the part further from the fracture; and be your operation in the stricture of the envelopment and in mollification of that according to that we have said in the first envelopment below.
And then pause between the envelopment of stopa lion or cloth with which be equalized the tortuosity of the fracture, if in it is tortuosity; and if not, do not pause in it any cause.
And then pause upon it another bandage, and then bandage over these envelopment stars decently made forthwith, if in the place there is no inflammation or pus caut, and then pause upon it that which seats the pus that and that oste the inflammation; and leave that for some days; and then you strengthen upon it the stars.
And be the stars of the majority of wide cane race, and well formed wisely; or be of pine wood; or of palm ramps; and be the ample of each star according to this form and this figure:
However cover that the star that is paused upon that fracture be thicker and wider a little than the other stars, and the length of the star according to that is legible to the limb in magnitude and smallness; and then strengthen upon the stars with another bandage according to your first stricture; and then lie below with faithful decently according to that we have said of stricture, and it is that be your stricture according to the place of the fracture most, and as much as it is elongated of the fracture, is less the stricture.
And make sure that the threads are of medium thickness and subtlety, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the operation of some fools placing their threads of cords of the rope, and it is a great error, when the stricture is outside equality.
And excessively fine threads are not convenient, because you will not be able to constrict with those what you want, and it is necessary that between the stitches there be at least a finger's width.
And if the patient is said by the extremities of the limbs near the stricture in the places, then place those of wool or carded wool inside so that it does not cause him injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a plague, and an accident occurs in the spine, and I will come with a remembrance in that in the second chapter singularity.
And know that every broken limb is not straight with stitches on the first day; the reason is that the limb, when it is large, should not be placed upon it with stitches, but rather after about five or seven days, or more, according to your certainty of the advent of pus.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients restored, which are placed upon fractures and dislocations and twists.
The manner of plaster as a method of restoration of a fracture, convenient for many complexities and mostly for women and children, because it is of that which does not bring heat or friction: and it is that you take of powdered meal, and it is the marrow of the flour that depends from the movement of the mill on the sides of the mill.
And mix that thus, unless you sift it with egg white, and place the mixture of it not too thick nor subtle; and then use that.
Another way to plaster a fractured, dislocated, and twisted limb: take asafoetida, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugwort, which is the root of wild millet, of all one drachma x; of frankincense and aloe one drachma v; aloes one drachma xx; all be ground and sieved and mixed with water, or with the white of an egg if the patient's complexion is cold; and then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores slightly broken bones, and confers strength to many men.
Another way to plaster a fracture and sprain: take mugwort and white althea, one drachma x; of frankincense and aloe one drachma v; of acacia, one drachma vi; of Armenian bol, one drachma xx.
All should be triturated in a mortar with much contrition, and sifted, and comfit with water or egg white and be administered.
Method of plaster to joints and separated bones from their places, and soothes pain that occurs in those and in fissures and fractures: take wool felt and submerge it in strong wine and boiled oil and place it over the location.
But more in this plaster there is not virtue of restoration, of pus coming out and expelling pains properly.
Another manner of plaster that restores broken bones: take leaves of fig tree and leaves of wild poppy; conter them all and make a plaster of them.
A shortened manner of plaster administered that deserves the restoration of bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remaining pus: recipe of root of altea, of chamomile and violets flowers, of ers flour, of all ana part .i.; All should be triturated and comfit with wine, if the limb is not scalded.
But if it is scalded, then mix that with moist coriander water or with water and use of it.
Another manner of plaster more strong in resolution than this first, when it occurs hard pus that deserves the restoration of the limb: recipe of root of altea and of seed of li, and of balaustia, and melilot, majorana, violets flowers, of chamomile, of all ana part i engual; all should be triturated and comfit with water of sauze or with sweet water or with wine, all according to the quality of the limb and the rest of the color of it.
But Ipocras does not say in his book that it be placed over the broken limb that deserves its restoration, but rather made of wax and oil and no other cause; and recommends that it be of medium thickness.
But Gualia has seen that they be placed over the broken limb that deserves its restoration and the causes in which is dryness with little heat so as mirra, incense and similar to those causes that we have recommended.
Method of plaster that soothes injury and pain: recipe of migat, of cezes, of human hair unshorn or feathers of bird, and of altea, and salt, parts equal; should be triturated and sifted and comfit, and be made plaster.
But the quantity according to which the bandage should remain and then be untied is that you consider if no pain or numbness or the bone is broken, it is not bad of its place, then you do not untie for many days. And if pain, numbness, or inflammation occurs in the place, then you should either loosen or untie it at that hour, and place a poultice on it.
And take a cloth of linen and a sponge of sea lion, humid, and submerge it in lukewarm water, and with that wash the place until the numbness and pain are appeased; and then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place on it wool cloth submerged in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and tie it on for a night until you are sure of a cauterized abscess, and that it rests the inflammation of the limb, and the pain of it is mitigated.
And then repeat that bandage, subtle and small poultice, and do not tighten it with your first constriction, and subtler with it until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, the redness, the pain, the inflammation, and all the accidents are already mitigated from all, and indigested of poultice and bandage, then you repeat that as you have done at the beginning, equally.
And if no accidents occur to the limb of those that have been said, then do not untie it until three days or four or five or seven. And sometimes all this is left for twenty days according to what appears to you for the unbandaging of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh over it.
And then adjust in the bandage more than in your first bandage, and adjust in the thickening of the food of the patient, according to what precedes the remembrance of it.
But if you see the place of the fracture already dry and thinner than it should, then you know that nourishment is prohibited from reaching it; and therefore embrocate it with lukewarm water, every time that you untie it; and let it be so for three days; and loosen the bandage a little, and by this operation, the nourishment reaches the limb, and it heals slightly.
But that which the foolish restorers of the fracture of the limb do another way when it is not restored at the beginning, according to what it should, and is restored according to tortuosity, is error according to the operation of theirs, and great fear, and if it were straighter, that which they enlarged the Ancients in their writings and warned with it.
And I have not seen any of them do this, because it is more reasonable that they do not do it.
Chapter Second: Of fracture when it occurs in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and their causes are distinct, because some fractures are made by the blow of a weapon, and the blow thrusts all the bones into the skull, which is left the bone, as if it were done in the shaft, and for this reason this species is called ascial; or the weapon incides some part of the bone, and incides the flesh of it and does not pass the incision into the back of it; and this species of fracture is called absolute levatio.
And the plague of these two fractures is either great or small, and of fracture that is contortion or attrition; and the cause of it is percussion with a blunt or a sharp object, and their likenesses.
And this fracture is either penetrating that already approaches the pannicle which is left the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures is either great or small.
And of fracture is that which is amalgamated in the bone, in the subtlety of the small hair, and for this reason this species is called capillary fracture.
And it is fracture that is made either by a sharp object or by percussion of a sharp object and their likenesses, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter the parts within, and it is made in the concave place as if it occurred to the payroles of eram when it occurs to their percussion and it is the part of their entrance into the parts within.
And mostly this is done in the bodies of moist bones, as if they were the heads of infants.
And to all these species of fractures, there are pains as if they were stars separated. And I will come with remembrance of all this in its place.
And we shall know all these species of fracture by detection upon them and by inquiry of them by means of touch and examination of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And the capillary species is known by this that it is discovered upon the bone, and it is abstergit, and lined upon it with touch, because the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of fracture, consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and then if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification upon pain, as it is vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and abscess of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and swelling of the joints and redness of them, and likenesses to them in the greater dispositions, adopt that it is not healed.
And if you see non-current accidents, you hope for health, then you begin the cure of it.
The cause is that if the patient has come to you at the beginning as he is sick and this is in the days of winter, then you should study the structure of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you should study the structure of the bone before the seventh day, because what is left of the bone should be corrupted by the panniculus, and that the accidents that we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and it is in small pieces and attrition, then you should insert the fractured and rubbed part, according to what I will tell you.
And you shave the head of the sick patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as you possibly can according to the figure of the wound, and that it be light on the patient.
And if it happens to you to detect the bone, flow of blood or pus, then you oppose it with what you have, and that is you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and olive oil, and make the wound rest until the pus is settled, and if you are sure of the blood flow; and then begin in the circuit of the bone and in its ablation.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.
And one way is that you insert the bone with a straight chisel, and this is the shape of it: And then administer another chisel this, a little wider than the first; and this is the shape of it:
And then administer the wider chisel than the second.
And you should have a number of chisels of various sizes, some wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are at the end of the sharpness of their extremities, and are of Indian iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the chisel so that it does not break the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you should perforate in its circuit before you administer the chisels with the drills, which are called non-deep drills.
And they are not called that otherwise because those do not reach the term of the bone of the skull to what is below it, for because the drill has a rounded extremity or margin on that which is left the head of it sharp resembling a margin or a small circle, which prohibits it from being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a large number of which as many as convenient to the thickness of each bone, so that it be to you present in all parts of the skull the extremity of which is sharp, the quantity in length and brevity be according to the quantity of thickness of this part.
And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is that you place the drill on the bone and revolve it with your hands until you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the drill to another location, and place it between all the trace the quantity of the thickness of the ray or environment of it.
And then insert with chisels that which is between each trace of the bone, and make it as subtle as you can on it in the end of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause for some instruments which you will have prepared for it, as such as tongs and subtle forceps.
And you should keep with all caution that the chisel does not touch any cause of the scalp.
And therefore, as you will have circled the bone perforating, and if it is continuous with it, and you have extracted it, you should shave and level that in the roughness of it of that which is in the bone with another instrument resembling a chisel, but however, it should be thinner and more subtle than the others chisels.
And if any pieces of the small bones and splinters remain, then take that with ease with that which you have of the instruments; and then take care of the wound with liniments and ointments which we have said.
And the other way of operation is lighter, far from pain, which says Gualia and the lauzec for great praise; and this is the sermon of it: It is necessary to you at the beginning that you begin to discover the part of the bone of the place in which is the fracture most vehement and most manifest, so that when you have discovered that part and you have placed below it the extremity of this chisel lent and agile of which this is the form:
And it is that the lenticular part is long and you do not insert any cause, and the sharp part is of it in two sides forward in length, as sometimes; that the lenticular part be adjusted to the scalp with the face of it incident acute in the bone.
And then strike upon the chisel of one part with a small mallet until you insert all the bone with ease as it is revolved, and you are sure of the panniculus that no accident happens in it that you fear in any way, and if the medicant14 was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
And now if any cause annexed to the bone of the panniculus in some places of bone, insert that of it with the extremity of that lenticular chisel, and deliver that of it with ease, because it is delivered of it its harm and its pain.
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate into the panniculus, and what is separated or taken from the bone is the fascia of it, and remains in it roughness and subtle satagias, then when you remove that roughness and eradicate those satagias with subtle razors with which you have prepared the number, of diverse quantities so that it be possible to administer them in all places the most convenient of them and the most straight of them according to what in which you are losing your operation that broken bone and the figure of it.
And so that you administer in your reason with the tool with which you shave the bone at the beginning the most blunt of those razors, and then use the most subtle of it, and do not cease doing so according to continuity until you reach to administer the most thick and the most subtle of all of them.
But in any of the other fractures, small and subtle, so that you administer care according to what you perceive for the rectification of it.
And it is the cause that it is not  mastered over all those who have studied in that art. And this is about what we have shown in great fractures.
And if the panniculus is cut when you eradicate the bone, then so that you take cloth from it according to the quantity of the wound, and submerge that in rose oil, and place that over the orifice of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place that over the first cloth and do this as lightly as you can, so that you do not injure the cephalic; and then administer over that bandage, and do not tighten it except for the quantity that the wound has alone.
And leave that with this care for a day or two until you are sure of the abscess coming out; and then untie that and then administer medicines with which is superfluous dryness, as well as roots of flax and flour of errs, and powder of incense and of aristolochia, and similar to those.
And that you make of these medicines powder that be pulverized over the wound, as well as they are dried; and universally, administer in that all medicine of property of which is that it abstergesca and do not bite.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and that there be no in it filth of rinsing or other; nor do you allow virulence to be added to it; for when it is adjusted over the panniculus of the brain, it corrupts that and you can corrupt that, and happens to the patient what evil malice.
And happens in the cephalic of the head, as of it is modified the bone and most when is lessened the cure of it, and it is blackness on the surface of it; and therefore consider then if happens to the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is pernicious all adopted.
And if it does not happen blackness but of the medicine that is placed over it, and it is in virtue of that medicine that makes that blackness, then so that you take of honey one part and of rose oil parts three, and in those liniments a cloth, and then place that over the cephalic; and then cure that with the species of cure that convenes until it be healed.
Chapter III.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken but two parts together, or one of those some times, for in it you have two bones, for the part left is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and does not happen to it but attrition and tortuosity and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then so that you introduce the said little one in the one's nose, and that you align that fracture inside with your said finger, and with the said point outside, until the nose returns according to its natural figure. And be done that by you with facility, and guard yourself that no cause by that your operation over the patient pain.
And if the fracture is in the most sovereign part of the nose, and the said one does not reach to that, then so that it be equal with the extremity of the ray in which it is little thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the semblance of that.
And take care of you in the restoration of it in the first day of the fracture, if it is possible; and if not, then about the seventh or tenth of the repose of the abscess coming out.
And about introduce in the extremity of the nose a piece of cloth of it, if the fracture is in one part, or put two splints, if the fracture is in two parts, and be in the splint thickness of such quantity that it fills the trough of the nose.
And say some of the ancient restorers that the splints be enclosed in border, and be changed in every day, but it is not seen to me; have that the piece be enclosed in album of hen comfit with powder of moli; and then leave the qualgua until the bone be set and be hardened the cartilage.
And some times be introduced in the nose, in place of splint, pena of auca pus that is enveloped of cloth of the lion; and be that already you the fracture of the nose most vehement and that the patient not be prohibited of inspiration.
And this cause is not to you necessary; and if you want, do that, and if you want, do splints.
And if another abscess occurs after cautery, apply a plaster with cerot or a cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if no abscess occurs after cautery, then cover the area with a plaster made of sesame flour and powdered quince seeds with egg white; and place a linen cloth over it, and do not touch it with any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then cover those with a cloth, and remove them with instruments that are suitable for those; and then close the fissure, and cure it with a liquid that mends and consolidates, and with plasters suitable for that purpose.
And if a plague occurs in the nose, then treat it with poultices and administer lead pipes until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture of it is not accompanied by a plague, look then if the fracture is only outwardly, and is not broken in two and curved at the parts inside, then its cognition is lighter.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, have you introduce your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left mandible, then introduce the right index finger; and push with that the mobility of the fracture inside with ease to the outer parts, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; with that decency equal them.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated in two, then have you administer extension of two parts according to rectitude until it is possible the equation of it.
And if there occurs a commotion of teeth to disruption, then strengthen those of which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or lead; and place over the broken jaw cerot, and place over it a doubled cloth, and place over the cloth a large splint made decently, or a piece of sole equal to the length of the jaw; and tie that desus according to how you are equipped the binding of it and that the application is convenient, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use of rest and tranquility, and put the food of him soft and boiled.
And if you estimate that already is altered the figure in any manner, then you take off and untie that at the third day; and then rectify what is already altered of it and plaster it with powder of wool, with egg white, or with sesame flour, because you will have removed the cerot from it, and place over the plaster wool.
And thus when it stands annexed and adjusted that plaster over it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, that you do not untie it until it is healed and strengthened the fracture; because many times this fracture is straightened in three weeks.
And if another time occurs abscess after cautery, then administer what we have said many times in the sedation of it until it is healed, and be removed that abscess.
And if the fracture is with a plague, then consider if it is a piece of the bone separated or stitches, then you subtile that you remove those stitches with what you consider appropriate in the removal of them from the instruments.
And if the orifices of the plague are strait, then you enlarge it with the fleubotom according to the quantity of your necessity.
And when you will have removed those stitches, and of those there is no sir removed, then you close the orifices of the plague if it is wide; and if not, place over it one of the plasters that you consider appropriate and cover it, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times is broken the clavicle from the side before the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
Because it is broken, or separated, or is separated in two parts one that does not occur stitches; and it is easy to the restoration of it; or it is so that it occurs in it stitches, and it is difficult to the restoration of it; or it is so that the fracture is with a plague.
And the operation in that, when the fracture is with a plague, is that you make to be present two ministers, so that one has the adjutori and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck with that part.
And then engulph the fracture with your fingers until it is made the form of it according to what you cover, and that there is no eminence little nor concavity.
And if you needed more extension, then have you placed below the nipple of the patient a support of cloth or wool, and be the greatness of it according to the quantity of your necessity; and extend and elevate the clavicle and bind the support with your hand until you engulph the fracture according to what you cover.
And if you cannot pull the extremity of the clavicle to the outer parts, because it is arrived at the preon, then have the patient be propped up on his neck, and place over the muscle of it a cushion medium in greatness.
And the minister presses the muscle of it to the lower parts until the bone of the clavicle is elevated that is in the preon in its, and then you rectify the fracture and engulph that with your fingers.
But if you feel pains in the collarbone and it is painful, then you do what you do on that and remove those pains with ease.
And if it is held in the bone, then your ingenuity makes an incision of it with one of the scissors which you have equipped for this, because you had placed below the collarbone the instrument that serves the chisel; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is the form of it:
And it is similar to a chisel of which this is the concavity; and its width according to what you have need of it, according to the size of the bone or the smallness of it.
But the length of it be according to what is possible to you in the operation; that it have two extremities thus as sometimes, and one extremity be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the plague which you have incised when you have extracted the pain from the bone is wide, and it is certain of pus caut, then you adjust the two lips of the plague with suture.
And if the disruption is little, or you fear pus caut, then you plug the plague with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the scissura of the plague.
And if pus caut happens, then you put a cloth in vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it on top.
And all, that it not be painful the plate above the broken bone; and for so that you see the patient every day, that when the ligament is softened or sometimes the plate already be remauguda, then you rectify that and strengthen the ligament.
And then put the sleep of the patient on the neck of it, and put below the tip of it of his sleep in the night a small cushion; so that with that be elevated the assistant of it from the place of that, and therefore is elevated the fracture of the collarbone to elevation of the muscle; or be the arm of it tied to the neck of that.
And do not untie the ligament if you do not happen in the place promptly or pus, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it to that, and adjust in the stricture and restore that and let it within that it is restored and be coagulated the fracture of the collarbone.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the measure in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is made in less time than that.
Chapter VI.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula tart is broken in the place of the side of it and not broken of it but the edges.
And therefore when it is broken the place of it — and it is broken in the middle place of it — and it is not known but by touch, then according to what is the figure of the fracture forces you the preparation of it and reduce that according to the natural figure in all manner of engine the which is to you possible.
And then put on the place powder of mill with egg white and linen cloth; and then put powder of cloth doubled; and then put on it plate of subtle table according to the quantity of the spatula all or maybe a little.
But if below the plate is concavity in some of the places of the spatula, then you fill that concavity with linen cloth, and then put the plate according to equality.
And then strengthen above with humidity long with constriction decent and with firmness of the plate within that it not be remauguda from its place.
And consider the ligament in each day; and all times that it is softened, tie another time and strengthen it and equal the plate if it is remauguda from its place.
And put the patient on his side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days or in twenty-five.
And therefore when this number is completed, then you dissolve the ligament, and that you know for sure that that is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone pes and put below the codena, then you split on it, and remove it, and do according to what we have said in the fracture of the collarbone of sadness of pus caut if it happens here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle of it and that must be set; but the extremities of it must be moved that they may be cicatrized and comminuted.
And of the accidents of it when the middle of it is broken, it is that it declines to the parts of it and happens to it great pain, and difficulty in breathing, and cough, and strong fever and vomiting of blood; and it is declared in it the cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that it be laid the patient on his back, and place between the spaces of it a cushion; and then press on the muscles of it, and adjust the bones of it with the hands of two sides, and subtly in the operation of it and in the equation according to every manner that is ad this possible and that convenes ad this, until that returns the figure of the bone according to that cove.
And then place on it plaster and lint and place above the table of a subtle of gauze or of caligi and of their semblance in levitat, because that you envelop that in cloth; and then subtly in lian that over the broken bone that it not be harmful; and go to ligation according to rotundity to the back sometimes, and strengthen that with stricture decent; and then consider the ligation in every hour, and when it is softened strengthen that.
And if necessity provokes the desliament of that, you must corrode or pruziment, the pain, the abscess that happens in the place, then you cut and untie that and eradicate the plaster and rectify that which happens by that in manner of cure of it; and then reduce the plaster, if times to that the face; and adjust to it stricture until that it be healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of the back when it is broken.
Know that fracture of the back does not catch but in places thick that follow the back; and of the extremities of them of the part before, it does not happen to those contricio but by that because that they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of it is not hidden to the feeling, therefore the inquiry of it with the said.
And the restoration is so that you align the fracture with the said according to the manner possible, until that it be aligned according to that cove, and then plaster and strengthen the broken bone with a splint, if it is needed of that.
And if the fracture of the back is declining to the parts of it, then happens to the patient great pain and puncture, as well as puncture that happens to that one who has pleureci, and because that the bones put the veil; and happens to it difficulty of breathing, and cough, and vomiting of blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the Ancients were ingenious in that with many ingenious things.
And of theirs that is said: he who places foods of the patient that engendered inflammation and flatulence, because that the belly be inflated and be extended, and the fracture be impelled to the parts outside.
But we would not recommend that because that it not be cause lighter to generate abscess caut, if it does not happen; and if it already happens, then it already helps to that.
And they said some of them that it be placed over a windpipe and then that it sucks with force; and it is seeming to ratiocination, but however it is feared that the windpipe will drive the superfluities to the place by the weakness.
And they said some of them: he who the place be covered with wool that already is submerged in hot oil, and that be placed powders between the back until that it be filled, that it be the ligation aligned when it envelops that according to rotundity; and then be cured the patient with cure of pleurisy of food and of medicine.
And if vehement cause constricts the patient the one that does not suffer, and the bones put the veil with puncture leading, they fear over the patient, then he who cuts over the place, discover from the broken rib, and then place over that instrument that conserve the ciph, the remembrance of which is preceded, and that they cut the bones with facility, and extract that.
And then that they adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sewing, and cure that with plasters until that it be healed.
And if it happens of the abscess, then you cut and put powders in hot oil and place over the place, and that it be cured the patient with that which cove to the abscess of the parts.
And be laid on the side on which he sleeps, until that it be healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fracture of the vertebra of the back and of the neck.
Sometimes it happens that fracture to the bones of the neck, and then it must be set to them, and seemingly to the vertebrae of the back. And as that happens to some and wants to know if it will heal or not, then consider, because if sometimes their hands already softened and stupefied and dead and cannot more those nor extend nor strengthen those and when you constrict those with your said or prick those with a needle and no feel in those pain, then you know that it is not healed, and in the worst dispositions it is mortal.
And if you feel those and constrict those with the said and puncture, then you know that the marrow of the bone is saved and that the patient will cure with cure.
And if you see in the neck vertebrae the appearance of this and you want to know if it is healing or not, then consider the feet of him.
Then if you see that they are already softened, attend to those humors that are occurring in the hands, and when you touch on their back, you feel a windiness and digestion, and sometimes it is raised on its back and wants to urinate and cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and therefore do not work in the cure of him.
But if you do not see any cause of those, the cause is better and less severe.
And the cure of him is that you pierce the seat of the abscess cautery and place it over the affected vertebra, rubbed with olive oil or with hot poultices; place over it sometimes during the day until the abscess is cauterized.
And then place one of the comforting and existing plasters, and strengthen over it with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and that he does not sleep on the part with which he feels pain until it is healed.
And if you see the separation of the bone fragments or any cause that is already separated from him, then cover that with a bandage and that you hold that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then cure that with some ointment of the herbs, until it is healed.
But if the tail bone is broken, and it is the end of the pelvis, then cover that with a bandage and that you hold that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with suture if it is large; and then cure that with some ointment of the herbs, until it is healed.
And if you feel fragments of bone in him, then cover that, and hold that, and cure the wound with the cure that has preceded until it is healed.
Chapter X.
Of the Restoration of the Womb.
The bones of the pelvis are broken, and if they break, then the fracture of them is nothing but a communication in the extremities of those and they are incised in the length and decline to the parts of the sides; and you see pain in the place and puncture, and the leg is stunned when the fracture.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture as is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is in the extremity of it so only, then you align it according to how it is apparelled to you by equation align until it is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long or that already declines to the parts below, then you make the patient lie on his belly until it is prepared to you the restoration of that fracture. And therefore when you have aligned it, place over it plaster, and then place over it a bandage of wool or of linen, and strengthen by stricture over which you do not fear puncture of fracture nor removal of the splint.
And align the concavity with what fills it until that presses the stricture according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on his back or over his side.
But if you see an abscess cauterize, then you cease from extension of it and its restoration until the abscess is cauterized, and place over it what sits it according to what has preceded; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it thus as you cover.
And if you see in the bone fragments or they are comminuted, then you treat them.
Chapter XI.
Of the Restoration of the Fracture of the  Arm.
The arm is that which is between the shoulder to the hand.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, of which is one that you take a splint of medium thickness according to this form:
And there are ties in two extremities two bandages, and then it is suspended from the elevated place.
And follow the patient on a chair; and then throw out his broken arm over the splint until it reaches the tip of it in the middle part of which is annexed the curvature of the splint.
And then suspend over it some heavy cause, or the minister extends it to the parts below; and then the doctor and that the fracture with his hands simultaneously, until the fracture returns according to what is covered.
And the other way is that the patient be raised on his neck and suspend the arm of his collar with a bandage; and then command two ministers that one of them holds that which is above the fracture with his hands; and the other holds the pus below of it; and each of them extends to its part.
And if you want the extension to be more strong, then you strengthen over the fracture with a bandage and over that with another bandage, and each of the two ministers extend to their two parts.
And therefore if the fracture is near the extremity of the muscle, then cover that the half of the bandage is placed over the tip and the other below the fracture of the joint.
And similarly if the fracture is near the joint, then cover that the bandage is placed over that place and over that joint.
Gently guide that and its work, and as soon as it is guided according to how it is covered and applied for good application, then strengthen it so that it does not happen after a boil caut.
But if a boil occurs, then leave the constriction for seven days, and place upon it wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the boil rests, and then strengthen it.
And the manner of strengthening it is that you place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm upon that support, and place your hand open over its muscle.
And place the cloth and strengthen over the support the arm so that the arm be in place of stars, unless any cause prohibits this from the broken bone.
And then use of stars.
And it is that you place upon the fracture that star which be pus wider and pus stronger than the other stars; and place between each star the width of the finger, and be the length of the stars according to the fracture with addition of three fingers of all part.
And then strengthen over the stars with the constriction which I have said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your constriction over the place of the fracture be more strait. And as much as is lengthened the fracture be the constriction lesser.
And therefore if you see the situation of the stars and the constriction as we have said in the hour in which you restore the limb to its situation, then you do.
And if you fear a boil, then you leave the constriction and the stars for seven days as we have said.
And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days so that nothing happens in the place proximate or inflammation; or is prohibited the food to come to the limb by disposition of superfluity of constriction; and therefore rectify all that as we have said.
But if you are upon confidence that nothing will happen from this, then do not untie the ligature until after three days or more.
And be the sleep of the patient upon their back, and their hands upon the stomach.
And place upon the support a cushion full of wool guided, and consider in all hour of the night or of the day that not be untied nor dissolved the figure of the broken limb, or that be the ligament softened, therefore rectify that all with your virtue.
And place the food of the patient according to the order which we have before said, in such a way that the food be subtle in the beginning until it is begun to strengthen; and then cover that the food of it becomes thicker.
For of custom of the support and of the bandage of it is that they be strengthened in forty days; and then cover that they be untied and be administered bath and be medicated with unguents those which rectify that.
And if the fracture is laid smooth, then you do not untie from that the ligature and the stars within a day or within two months.
Chapter .xii.
Of the Restoration of Fracture of the Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two forearms; of which one is small, and that is the one that sits the finger well, and the other is large and that is the one that is placed below the little finger, from the part further down.
And very often is broken the major forearm alone, or the small one, and very often they are broken together.
And when is broken the small forearm above, then the restoration of it is lighter, and the healing of it is lighter.
And when is broken the forearm of it below the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse of that is when are broken the two bones together.
Therefore if the bone which is broken is the small forearm above, then it is convenient to the physician, he must the restoration of it, that place small extension with ease until that be guided.
But if the large forearm is that which is broken, then cover that place extension more vehement.
And if the two forearms are together broken, then cover that you place the constriction more strong.
And cover that you place the figure of the hand, the restoration of it and the extension of it extended over a cushion, and be the wrist of the hand at the parts above, more out than all the other fingers, and the little finger be lower than the other fingers, and the patient be centered upon a pillow laid.
And be a cushion in front of it in the height or straight of it, so that the patient does not hunch over work.
And then the minister extends the arm from the part further down with his hand or ligament, and another minister extends above similarly.
And then the physician guides the bones until he reduces that according to the best figure that he can.
And if in the facture of the bones are splints, then you pierce to reduce all that in its place with your virtue.
And if appear in it a splint separated that punishes the codena and is not at your hope in the restoration of it, then you finish upon it and oust that according to the manner which we have said below.
But if the fracture is with a plague, I have already made a singular chapter about that, and therefore take the cure of that.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then line a cloth with cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and make it between thickness and subtlety, and strengthen the cloth above it with a subtle structure until the abscess is subsided; and then remove the cerate and place a prepared plaster of powdered mold with clear of egg, and then place stars; and let the star be the one that is placed on that fracture pus later and stronger.
And know that the name of the stars of the arm is seen in the major dispositons, or whether the fracture is in one focus or in two foci together.
And then place your structure over the place of the fracture, pus strong and pus vehement, and when more it is raised the extrictura to the parts above the fracture or to the parts pus basses of it, place the structure a little more soft according to that is preceded the rememoracio of it in the beginning of the capitol.
And the cloths that are enveloped over the fracture shall be soft cloths, moistened, and not hard nor rough, and the thread with which it is strengthened shall be of its own proper thickness between subtle and gross, as we have recommanded.
And consider the limb and the ligament after some days. And if there occurs any cause for which rectification is necessary, so as prudently the one which occurs to the limb, and then cover that you embrokes the limb with water cauda until that ceases that prudency.
And let the limb not be constricted until it rests, and then return the structure.
And if the structure is already softened and the bone is already remaugut and seems like those, then rectify all that with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited from reaching the limb by superfluity of structure, for then cover that you soften it a little and let it for some days until it runs to it the nourishment, and then strengthen it.
But if to the patient there occurs no cause of those that we have said, then cover that it not be dislocated within about 20 days or thereabouts of those.
And then let the hand of the patient be suspended by its virtue of movement agitatius, and make him sleep upon his two.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or in 32 days, or very well it is restored in 28 days, and all that is according to the disposition of the compleccios and the dispositions of the virtue.
Chapter XIII.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and the fingers.
Know that to the thumb of the hand and to the knuckles of the fingers, tart ends must fracture, and do not occur to them but attrition many times.

Chapter XIV.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the bone of the hip.
The bone of the hip many times is broken and is declared to the sentiment according to that it is converted to the part in front and to the most sovereign.
And the restoration of it is made in such a way that the ligament is strengthened over the fracture and another ligament below the fracture, and the patient be prostrate upon his belly and upon his face.
And then the minister extends the entire ligament to its parting according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is located at the root of the thigh, then the ligament is pulled towards the denticle at the parting of the pelvis, so that the extension falls upon it, and the other ligament is left at the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is located at the denticle, then the ligament is pulled towards the denticle, and the extension is placed at the parting.
And then the doctor aligns the bones with both hands until it is reduced according to the natural likeness and the bone is joined with a good connection; then cover it with plaster and a splint, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs there, then you leave it for a few days until the abscess has cauterized, and then return to your cure.
The splint is made like this: cover the fracture with a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and remove any superfluity; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the thigh and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long pin between the thigh and the leg from the denticle to the parting, and the ends of the pin come from the sovereign parting of both parts, and then roll over the leg and thigh what superfluity of the bandage.
And then place on the thigh in the place of that fracture, splints, and place one splint on the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the splint is coagulated.
And then begin with a strong splint on the middle of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and as much as you move away from the ligament of the fracture, your splint should be smaller and lighter and softer.
And then extend both ends of the pin to the parting until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and strengthen both ends of the splints on the other side so that the ligament is not removed from its place.
And then leave the splint on it, so that no corrosion, abscess cauterization, or inflammation or similar things occur in the limb; and if any of these things occur, then remove it carefully from there, and rectify what has happened as we have said many times.
But if the bone is broken in a way that it cannot be aligned, then you should align it as much as is possible; and if not, then cover it and remove that, and care for it with what has preceded the memory until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is strengthened with something else that is adjusted to it, like we have said in the adjutori and the arm.
But however, in the restoration of it in this manner, do not cause the patient lameness; and if it is restored alone, then apply something else to the other leg, then it is necessary that he limp from the one that is always.
And know that the thigh is strengthened in 40 days or a few more or a few less, and all this is according to the diversity of the complexities and other dispositions.
Chapter XV.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap of the thigh.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap of the thigh, often occurs, but it happens many times with a tearing.
But if a fracture occurs, then it is not but a fissure or a crack in its parts, and this is with a plague or a set of plagues, and above all this is the state with the feeling.
And the restoration of it is in this way that it is aligned what is broken of its parts with the said ones until it is joined and joined according to what is possible by equation and facility and fabrication.
And then place plaster and place on it a round splint, if you need it; and strengthen it with a splint as convenient.
And consider all the dispositions which we have recommended in the other fractures, as abscess cauterization and similar to those, so that it opposes all accidents to what is convenient until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is thick, and it is named by the name of the leg; and the other is thin, and it is named fibula.
And it happens to some kinds of fractures that happen to both bones of the leg, and for that reason the restoration of it is made as the restoration of the arm, and the operation of it is one.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is converted to all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is converted to the front parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, it bends behind, and it is of those causes that are not to your liking.
And then administer extension and equation and ligation of the splints according to that alignment.
But however, if the fracture of the leg is recent and has many bandages, that the extension be less and pus lighter, and alleviate with your virtue.
And in the leg there is some cause of operation on the arm, and when you tie the stars and that completes all your operation, then you will take pieces of wood of pine, which is placed below the causes which are placed in soles, and are made by shoots of palm; some of those which are of small thickness, and are not of the branches, and the length of those according to the length of the leg, and from the bone inside to the pus below.
And then envelope on each strip in two envelopes according to the length of it, and place one on the part of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, and be from the bone inside to the bottom of the foot.
And then tie the two straps in three places of two extremities and in the middle place, because with this structure the leg is prohibited from bending to the right and left side, and it stands for good station.
And already is administered a splint of wood according to the length of the leg; and be placed in it the leg so that it conserve that of movement.
And mostly, so that you do this in a fracture, and when with that is plague properly.
And then consider in each day the leg and study with it by behind study, and you guard it from abscess and from inflammation or other causes similar; and when it happens any cause of those, then oppose yourself to it with what you should do before that it be healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the talus and of the bones of the foot and of the digits.
To the talus there does not happen fracture of all; but to the bones of the foot there happens fracture some times; and to the digits of the foot few happens fracture, and there does not happen to them but attrition in the middle of disposition.
And if to the bones of the foot happens fracture and sometimes those bones be elevated above each other, then the patient places his foot on the ground prepared as if they were going.
And then place one foot on what is elevated of those bones, and then step on those, and press, and tie those until they return to the places.
And then place plaster and stop above, and place below the belly of the foot a small table to which are two heads placed.
And then strengthen that with the foot with structure descent, because you have enveloped that with cloths and with other causes of which you have said.
And when to it pass three days or four, then you untie the ligament, because you find the bones tied, or are broken or are dislocated, and the operation in those is thus as you know.
But if some digits are broken, then restore those, and tie those as I and recounted to you in the restoration of the digits of the hand.
