And it is convenient to you that you do not forget that you do not consider all the causes which you have been told to you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have said; and oppose yourself to every accident with what you should.
Chapter XVIII.
Of fracture of the hip bone of the woman and of the bone of the pubic bone and of the groin of the man.
When the hip bone of the woman is broken, then make that cease in a bed laid down, and then incline that to the part of the back a little, and it be held behind.
And then the matron puts the hip bone of cloth, and be made in the hip of it as she hopes; and then the woman be supported, and elevate the back of her little by little, because all that is thrown away until it be before the part of the hip as she hopes and the broken bone returns.
And then place pillows on the back of her, and when she wants to urinate removes the cloth with facility and then urine, and return that according to the manner according to which you have seen that at the beginning, and then return to her first pillow.
And do thus for vii days or around those, because that is restored.
And if you want, take a bladder of sheep and strengthen over the wound of her a tube of cane; and introduce all the bladder in the hip of her, and then insufflate into the tube with virtue until the bladder be inflated within the hip, because the fracture returns.
And then be filled with cloth and stay for some days according to we have recounted, until it be healed.
And when the bone of the pubic of man or of woman is broken, then administer in the restoration and stitching of it what we have recounted in the bone of the hip.
And what is right is not hidden in these proper fractures and which few understand, especially to him who has some study and use and understands this my book well; for that fracture means to you in the major dispositions of the right way in the restoration and in the stricture of it, and therefore understand well.
But in the groin of the man when it is broken, take a cloth of flax, and put the groin in it; and then put a bandage over it, and wrap it, and leave it for three days or about those, and it will be healed.
Chapter XIX.
Of the restoration of bone fractures when it is plagued.
It is convenient for him to whom a fracture happens with plague, and especially if the bone is large as is the bone of the hip or of the humerus, or similar to that, that you take care and flebotomize him as soon as the conditions of flebotomy to you or testify as we have said above.
And if the plague flows blood, then you must take care to the retention of it as much as you can pulverize over it with a trill, if to you another cause is not present.
And then as soon as you begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not prolong that, if you do not happen to have an abscess.
But if an abscess happens, then you leave the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess is given, and do not approach it on the third day and the fourth of all, for you will cause to happen bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the hip uncovered, then you must take care to reduce it and align it with your hands with facility and with little extension.
But if the extension is not to you prepared and the equation of it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands with this instrument; and the instrument is made of iron, the length of which is of seven digits or of eight and the width of which is according to the quantity of the plague; and for that it is necessary to the physician that he prepares of those three or four according to the quantity that you indicate to it in the cure in all species of fracture.
And let it be rounded, and let it have little thickness so that it is not bent, the comprehension over it during the hour of the operation.
And let it be of acute extremity at which be margin in its extremities; and let the underside of it be of tending thickness, and of the majority of it within to the parts.
And this is the form of it:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", so it is the little palette.
Because it must be placed the extremity of it ascends elevated over the extremity of the bone prominent.
And impel it in one push while it returns the bone, and it is aligned with some equation.
And therefore strive to align the extremities of the fracture between them, and if the extremity of that broken bone is subtle, and does not take that instrument for good acceptance, then you must insert the extremity of that bone while the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone of all with that which we have recommended, then insert it with what you coney to it of the incisors which we have said; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever manner it is to you possible.
And then smooth that which remains in the bone of roughness and of small scabs.
And as the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient feels a violent and leading pain, then you know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and therefore do what you confer to the patient great benefit.
When is completed your restoration which you do to the bone, and then submerge cloth in black styptic wine, and properly if it is in summer, and do not place over the plague cerot or other cause in which there is oil, so that it does not cause in it to happen poultice and corruption.
And then administer stelas in the hour of your compliment of the restoration of the bone, and leave the plague uncovered as much as you insert the tract with forceps and uses of bandages according to the quantity of the plague.
And guard yourself with all caution that you do not strain the plague with the fracture, for many times have done this the foolish physicians, and have caused to happen over their patients death or sickness corrupt or ulcerated, so it is healed. And if your stricture is lighter, softer, different from the stricture of the other fractures.
And if the plague is great or is a bad plague, and you fear over that some bad accidents which we have recommended, and you feel pain in the place, then you must not place over it stelas, but make to it an envelope of hard cloths in place of stelas and strain it with those.
And when after a day or two the plague begins, that in it there be engendered brac, then you remove from under the cloth which you had placed over it with wine, and then administer poultices, with which of our custom it is that we medicate the plagues, as well as poultices tetrafarmacon and similar to that.
And it convenes to you that you untie the bandage and consider the plague in each day morning and evening until it is consolidated and fixed, and that the limb be prepared in such a manner that the poultice runs from it to the parts below with facility.
If many days pass with the plague and it is not consolidated nor is the arm separated from it, then you know that here are satagias of the small ones so that you inquire about the plague with a tenta. And of those satagias those that are separated remove and extract, those that from them are not separated and put the limb and make it occur pain, pierce you to cut those according to what to you is possible.
And if it happens to the plague a boil or ulcer, it is a stiomenios, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then you must put on each of those with what convenes to it of the cure of which is preceded narration in its chapter.
And of this in which you must know the best way and you make present your own: when the bone is broken, elevate the limb as if the bone of the thigh and the auxiliary and similar to those of the large limbs, and that you do not presume traction of it, nor the extraction of it for many times happens by this death; have left that within that you can, for very soon it will fall by itself in about 20 days or 30. And then cure the plague if it is seen, and if not leave that.
Chapter .xx.
Of the cure of nodules that happen in the vestiges of any fracture.
Many times happens this nodule in the vestiges of healing of fracture, and mostly that it is near the joints, and it is deformed by it the figure of the limb; and very soon prohibits the limb of its natural operation.
Therefore, consider if the nodule is recent and administer in that medicines that have stipulated as if it is aloen, and ensis, and mira, and sercacollo, and acrassia, and similar to those; in this way that you take from that some or all, and confess those with vi stiptic, or with album of egg, or with vi agre, and put that on the nodule in cloth, and constrict those on it by good constriction.
And leave the constriction that do not untie that for many days; and then untie that and iterate another, until that the nodule is removed; or strengthen on that lamina of lead descentment, for lead has the property of removing all that is inflated in the limbs.
But if the nodule is already in a worse converted and strict it is and necessity provokes to remove that coytadament, and then cut that by the part most sovereign of it, and incise that the superfluity and eminent, or rat the one with some rezoris until that it is removed; and cure that until that it is healed.
Chapter .xxi.
Of restoration of fracture when it is restored and then that which remains of the limb subtle and frail not according to its nature.
When a fracture of bone is restored, and then that which remains of the limb subtle and frail, then that is not done but for many causes: of which one is multitude of solution of the ligament and of the ligament of it made not according to what it should; or for superfluity of constriction of the ligaments until that the nourishment be prohibited to run to the limb; or for multitude of superfluous embrocation; or for superfluous movement not in its hour; or for paucity of blood in the body of the patient and for weakness of it.
And cure of that is to give to the patient to eat and make the heart of it tender until that the blood be multiplied in it; and administer baths; and intermet gaug and leticia upon it.
And then put a poultice on the limb, so that the poultice attracts to it much nourishment, or continue embrocation of it with aygua tebeza, until that the nourishment runs and returns to its natural figure.
Chapter .xxii.
Of the cure of bones broken when they are restored crookedly and prohibits the operation of them of that which it should.
When it happens to the limb already restored at the healing of it of tortuosity and eminence to the broken bone and nodule, and it is for that feeded of form of the limb, but however the limb is not prohibited of its natural operation, then you must not receive the word of that one who exists that member be of new broken.
But already there have been many foolish doctors and restorers the ones who have done that in our land; and this operation is very vituperable producing great fear the sign of which is mortal.
But if claudication and nodule are recent, then you must have them embrocated with aygua tebeza in which there are cooked mollifying herbs, as if it is the fuelas of altea and roots of it, and melilot, and similar to those; and be made a plaster with mollifying plaster as if it is diallaquilon well made; or that be prized mucillage of root of altea and be contused with gray of guanine or with oil of cirami, and be plastered with that; or that be prized fat figs and be trissated with fems of dove, and similar to those of the medicines that are named amermans consolidation.

Chapter XXIII.
Of the speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints of the limb, so that movement is prohibited, and the figure of the limb is distorted, and it causes great pain and injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to immediately attempt to reduce it, and not delay the reduction, because if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and it becomes very difficult to perform the dislocation reduction.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to be bad or stretched during the time of inflammation, because often it causes spasms and intense pain.
But when this happens, it is necessary that you apply a poultice to the patient's limb, and let it rest while the inflammation subsides; and then wrap the limb with warm water and oil, and then reduce it with ease.
And every limb should be treated with this so that the memory of the injury will come back to its place.
And you should already order the chapters on dislocations according to what was previously said about fractures; from the part of the body that is superior to the part that is inferior.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So dislocated the two mandibles if not tart; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: that they are slightly displaced from their places by a small movement, and they are softened a little; or they are dislocated by a complete and complicated dislocation, so that they are softened at the junction where the saliva of the patient flows and that he cannot hold it or close his mouth, and his tongue is enveloped in his word.
But when the dislocation of it is slight, then in the major dispositions it returns by itself with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and complicated, then it is necessary that it be administered a reduction of it gently, and not be delayed at all.
And that the minister have the head of the patient, and meanwhile the doctor puts his index finger in the root of the dislocation of the lower jaw from inside his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts his two fingers together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the others of his hands are outside with which he grasps; and he commands the patient to cease his mandibles and allow them to move from any part; and the doctor aligns the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if it is difficult to reduce it, and most of all if both mandibles are dislocated at the same time, then administer vaporization with warm water and oil, until it is made lighter the reduction of both and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And therefore, when they return and are aligned, and the mouth of the patient is closed and is not softened, then place on them poultices of cloth with cerot, and made of wax and oil rubbed; and then apply it with ease with bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be standing between two pillows, so that he does not strain either to the right or to the left; and do not force him to chew for any reason; having his food be forbidden to him, until when the pains are reduced and the mandibles are healed, then allow him to chew what he wants.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and spitting, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if it is difficult to reduce two mandibles when they are dislocated at any hour and are not converted to their places, then many times this happens fever and persistent diarrhea, and very often the belly of the patient is troubled, and very often and pure bile is vomited.
And when you see this, then you know that it is pernicious; and many times that person dies who happens to this within 10 days.
Chapter XXV.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and of the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the place inside by its continuation with the feet; and sometimes it is dislocated at the outer part, and this is declared to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then grasp the place with your palm with great force, so that it will return.
And then place on that plaster and poultices and strengthen that.
But the extremity of that which is connected and continuous with it, is not dislocated in any day; then it is necessary that it be reduced and aligned according to what has been said and according to what is at your disposal.
And then place on that plaster, and poultices, and a splint, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until it is healed.
And with this same cure is reduced the extremity of the muscle when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated from the part of the thigh to the part below.
And the second is that it is dislocated from the part above the muscle, and that is done hard; and it is not dislocated to the parts behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the parts in front by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and thrust out to the parts below the thigh, and mostly in those in which the flesh is few, it is easily dislocated; but in those in which there is much flesh, it is thrust out with difficulty.
And often accidents happen to some men by a blow or fall, and the muscle of them is inflamed with an abscess, and it is cured by that which is already dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you know this before you are over-certain of it, and then you will proceed in the cure of it.
And it is known the dislocation of it when it is in the parts below the thigh; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find between those a manifest diversity; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity and below the thigh the touch of the head of the muscle thus as a bowl, and the patient cannot reduce his hand to his armpit, nor move it at all.
If it is dislocated from the foot or above, and you then feel a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And from this dislocation is made a light reduction when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And the reduction of it is that the minister elevates his hand to the parts above; and then he places his thumbs below the thigh of him, and elevates the joint with strength upwards to its place, and the minister elevates his hand, and extends that upwards; and then he places that below where it returns lightly.
But if for what we have said it does not return, and it is from the beginning that the dislocation occurred, many days, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and administered a poultice that softens and liniments as if it were cotton wool of althea, and fennel, and melilot in water, and it be administered.
And then the patient be laid on his back and placed below the thigh of him, waiting for a wool that is equal between lightness and tightness, and the physician places his heel on the sphere, and elevates the head of the muscle with virtue, and pulls the hand of the patient so that it is not moved to the parts below, and another minister holds the head of the patient, so that it is not moved to the parts below, for that instantly returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it this way: and it is that you make a man present who is taller than the patient, and standing on the part of that side, and interweaves his muscle below the thigh of the patient and elevates the thigh of him to the parts above while the patient is suspended in air, and another minister pulls the hand of the patient from the parts below his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that with him that another cause be suspended, so that the grave one, for the dislocation returns instantly.
And it is reduced easily in another way: and it is that a long staff be placed on the ground, the head of which be round in figure, as a mortar, that it be neither thick nor thin, and then it be placed below the patient; because so that being placed on the head of the staff, cloths be placed on the patient, and the patient stand according to the length of the staff, and then the hand of him be extended to the parts below the other part; and the body of him be extended from the other part with strength, for the joint returns to its place with velocity.
And if it is a difficult reduction of it for all the causes that we have said, then use this cure: and it is that you take a staff the quantity of which be in the length of two arms, and the width of it be four digits, and the thickness of it be reduced in the front of the thigh according to this form:
And on the rounded head, be placed cloths of linen so that the staff does not hurt the patient.
And then it be placed below the thigh of the patient, and the hand or arm be extended on the staff into the parts below, and the staff be tied on the assistant and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm be placed on a step of a ladder according to the width, and the hand be extended to the parts below, and the other body be suspended from the other part, for the joint enters instantly.
And as the entering of it is completed according to whichever manner that is possible to you, then it is necessary that you place below the thigh a wool sphere of equal quantity; and then place a plaster prepared of powdered meal mixed with eggs and album of egg over the whole muscle, as if it were rolled upwards, and then a splint below the thigh with decent tightness; and the bandage be rolled over the plaster, and the hand of him be attached to the neck of him, and be left, and not be moved the hand of him for seven days.
And it is necessary that the food of the patient be little, while the member be comforted, for it is lighter to the healing of it.
And then the food should be given more near seven or five and exercise should be done; and therefore if it is stopped and not softened, then it is already healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times because of humidity that has come to it or for another cause, then it is necessary that in that place cauterization be administered with three burns, according to what was said before in the chapter on cauterization.
And if all this is done and after seven days the ligament is loosened and the joint is not held firm, and the bandage and the constriction are reduced over it several times, and it is not held firm and it catched and cannot raise that arm to the upper parts, then know that the nerves of it, which are at the head of the muscle, are already cut or extended or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
From the dislocation of that which is made with the foot and the knee or the parts behind, the reduction is made with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns.
And let the other constriction be administered there, and cure that until it is healed.
And if it happens that near the healing gravity falls on the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient uses baths many times until that gravity is relieved and it returns to its first nature.
Chapter .xxvii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the hip.
Know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and seems to return with difficulty; and that is dislocated to all parts, and more to the front than to the back, and its dislocation is not known to you until you are certain of the place or of the touch according to which figure it is dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated hip with its own place, that is to you declared manifestly, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor touch with that its muscle.
And you must reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before an abscess comes to it, because if it comes to it an abscess, it is difficult the reduction of it, and it is not healed of all; and more so when it is in the back parts, because it is worse of all the other kinds of dislocations, and of greater pain, and many times they die from it.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand of it be extended with two hands, and the arm of it be expanded, and the hands of the doctor be on the hip and below it, and he pushes the joint with the knuckles of his hands together, or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then it returns as if it bends the hand with its hand, until it strikes the hip with the root of its palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey the reduction, then administer vehement extension and a strong force.
And it is that two ministers extend the arm, and hold that raising of the ministers so that it not be remaugut from the extension.
And then it be revolved the arm from all parts, because it is enveloped a cloth folded long over the hand of it, or ligament.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, anoint that with some oil so that it be those causes which help in the lubrication of the joint with facility; and then pushes the joint with a vehement impulse until it returns.
And after the reduction of it, it is necessary that it be laid upon that plaster in which there is a sticking plaster and a concoction with album of egg, and it is extended with a descending constriction, and the arm is suspended from the neck, and it is left for some days.
And after it is loosened; and therefore if the joint is formed in its place, then you untie the ligament of it and leave it.
And if sometimes the joint does not become stronger, then you repeat the plaster and the ligament, and leave it for some days until it is extended; and after loosening it.
But if it happens to it gravity after the reduction of the joint, and the disposition of it is in movement, then administer moisture in baths and subtle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or rest upon the joint alleviated, so it is a matter of greasy motion; and then be bound, and leave it over the bones the day and the night. And after that, and put that in bath; and when it is dry, then you rub the joint with temperate friction, and then repeat upon that alleviated, so it is a matter of motion, once, and the second, and the third. Corruption has already arrived on the inside of the tooth, while it is still in the mouth.
And if you want, place upon that a piece of wet cowhide scraped with a knife and stretched over it; and do this several times, because that is humid and reduces it to its disposition.
Chapter .xxviii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the malassen, so it is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
Malassen of the hand some times is dislocated, and the reduction of the dislocation of it is lighter, and the contrary is of the other joints, but however it is necessary that it be velocity in the reduction of it in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place be apostematized or that anything happens to it an abscess.
And the reduction of the dislocation of that is that you place the joint of the patient's hand on a table and the assistant extends the hand of that, and the doctor places his hand or his palm on the prominence of the joint, and pushes that until it returns to its place.
But however, it must be considered if the palm is already placed in the palm of the hand, then the patient places the back of his hand on a table, despite the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is previous, then the position of the hand of that intrinsically on the table, so that the hand of the doctor rests on that prominence of the joint.
And therefore if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then strengthen that with a poultice, yielding the abscess and leaving it; and do not repeat this, for if not, you will not suffer nor can you perform the reduction of it more than days pass.
But however, the joint must be moved according to its tortuosity; and do not notice to the patient any cause, except that the hand is softened and cannot tighten any cause; then you know that the nerves are cut or torn, and then there is no way to heal it except that it be strengthened with a cautery near the location of it and firmly apply it, conferring any cause.
Therefore, when the bad limb is returned, then place on it the poultice which have recomposed, and let it be left and strengthened for 5 days; and then remove it and let it be left and the hand of it be exercised.
But if the movement of it is stayed and any gravity is placed upon it, then gently that with water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
Of the cure of dislocations of the said.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in every part.
And when a finger is dislocated from those at the back of the palm or in the palm of it, then stretch the finger and push the dislocation of it with your thumbs, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that from the part which is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend that until straight it is healed in that day.
And when night comes, bind that according to that narrative, and do not cease to unbind that in the day and exercise with movement, and bind that at night; and do this for some days until it is straight.
And do similar with this, if it is dislocated in the palm of the hand, and bind that to that part and do with this your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with this when it is dislocated in the other parts.
Chapter XXX.
Of the cure of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When a dislocation complicated occurs to any of the vertebrae of the back or of the neck, and the moved vertebrae are removed from their places, then in that there is no cure, for death befalls the patient.
And a sign of this is that the digestion of the patient is outside of his will, and he cannot retain that; and many times are softened some limbs of him or two legs of him or two arms of him, or one of those; or is removed a vertebra from its place, for many times is removed and many times is made the removal of those in four parts.
Therefore that is already removed to the parts behind and is called gelbositis.
But the cure of it is that then consider if the gelbositis occurs from puerility, for then in that there is no cure nor from that there is healing of all.
But in that which comes from a blow or from percussion or similar to those, already the Ancients have said the ways of cure with verbose sermons, of which the most returns with prophecy; but I have already and abbreviated of that of which the little excuse of the excess of it, for my best proximity to the better understanding exposition, with which I have this exposed and formatted from diverse instruments of that with which I will show this and expose that.
And of those there is no cure except that which occurs in the back properly with what I have recomposed.
And it is that the patient be extended on his face upon a bed near a wall, and be extended below him a soft instrument so that the feet of him are not lifted.
And then a straight stick be placed, fixed in the ground at the extremity of the bed near the head of him, and another stick in the aviro the feet of him at the other extremity of the bed.
And the assistant holds the stick, and do not be stopped in a ditch; and another assistant holds the other according to that form.
And then envelop over the head of the patient and below the tillic liamen leon firm; and extend the extremity of the liamen to the stick which is above the head of him, and bind that in it; and then strengthen the other liamen over the hips of him the denols of him, and below the legs of him; and then adjust all the liaments and bind those in the other stick which is above the feet of him.
And then the minister, having placed the patient's leg in the position of the staff, and not having moved the two staffs and ligaments from their places where they are placed, but however not being fixed as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with strength while returning, or who places upon that a table, and then that he adjust upon the table his feet while returning.
But if it does not return in this way, then he takes a table of which the width or length is in the ratio of three cubits, and hollows out in the wall of which we have said that it is near the patient a place in which enters the extremity of the table. And then he places the majority of the table upon the spine, and the doctor places his feet upon the other extremity, and tightens well while the spine is contracted and that it returns to its place.
And if you wish, open with a turn the one that is revolved with the hand; and that you fix on the ground the head of the patient in the rear of the pug two staffs, the length of which is of an arm, and you put the head of him in the rear of the pug also two staffs; and let the length that is between each staff be a palm, and already is made in each staff a hole in which runs the turn, and each one is held fixed on the ground so that it is not moved.
And let there be also a round staff in which is the turn, and let it be revolved in the two holes of the two staffs.
And in the extremity of it is a hole in which is formed the staff, the length of which is a palm with that which is revolved; and in the two other staffs is similar to that.
And then tighten the ligaments that you have straightened in the feet of the patient in the hole which is of his head, and the ligaments that you have straightened in the thighs of him in the turn which is of his feet.
And then you place each turn and stay a minister who twists the turn with his hand, and the doctor aligns the spine according to what we have said.
And as soon as the spine is returned, and the place is aligned, then you cover with a paste made with dried albumen of egg, and then with cotton; and then you place upon the paste a board of which the width is of three digits or about those, and the length of it is as much as it reaches the place of the hollow, and upon the part of the spine it lies.
And then bind it with the ligament which we have said, and use the patient of subtle nourishment while it is healing.
And if there remain any prominence in the place at the end of the healing, then it is necessary that medicines be administered which soften and cleanse with administration of the board for a long time, which we have recommended; and already it is administered in that place a plate of lead.
And some times an eminence occurs in the rear of the spines of the two, and it is cured by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already protrudes because it is not necessary that it be done proscription to it with this medicine, because very strong it will make you happen death.
Chapter XXXI.
Of the cure of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle should not be called dislocation only, and do not occur to those that occur to the other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways, because it is dislocated to the inside parts, or to the outside parts, or to the front parts, or to the back parts.
And mostly it is dislocated to the inside parts, and few to the front parts or to the back parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it to the inside parts is that, when you adjust the leg of the healthy patient with the patient, it is longer and the thigh is more prominent than of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his leg towards the knee, and it is the place that if the knee is positioned with a cauterized abscess, so that the head of the thigh has already arrived here.
And the sign of that one to which occurs dislocation is to the outside parts, is that the accidents of it are contrary to this accident.
And the sign of that one to which occurs dislocation to the front parts is that it expands its thigh according to its complement, but however it does not bend that which is in the thigh, and if it is forced to go and cannot go over that to the front parts; and the urine of it is retained and the groin of it is abscesses, and of the walking of it over the heel.
And the sign of that one to which occurs dislocation to the back parts is that it does not expand the thigh, nor can it bend that which bends the groin, and the thigh of it is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is of that place.
But the reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, that it is already fixed with that having it, then you administer reduction of it and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not in that cure of all; and therefore it is not necessary that in that one be done proscription.
But in the case of which the dislocation is recent, and is one of the four species of dislocation, then take hold of it so that you turn the joint and extend it to the inner and outer parts, and move it to the right and left, for it will return easily and does not require other care.
But if it does not return in this manner, then take that you prepare a strong minister and extend the leg of him from the back part, or with his hands, or with a bandage which is tied over the leg below the knee; and another minister extend the hand of that below so that he puts his hands below the buttocks of him; and then tighten with a bandage on the root of the groin, and hold another minister the extremity of the bandage.
And the extension of him or from the inner part to the shoulder or from the back part to the part of the loins.
And the extension of all those at once while the patient is elevated with his body from the ground and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is common to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for all species that we will say of the proper cure.
In the reduction of it proper, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is in the inner parts, then the patient must be laid down on his side; and then place the bandages on the root of the groin in that place which is between the head of the groin and the place that is below the shoulder; and then extend the bandage from the part of the shoulder to the highest part of the collarbone.
And then another minister firmly grasps the arms of him, and presses the thick part of the groin of the patient and extends to the outer parts by vehement extension, for that it returns to its place.
And this kind is of the lightest cure with which this limb is reduced than the others.
But if it is excused upon you and do not obey the entrance with this kind of cure of all, then take that the two legs of the patient are tied together with a strong, light bandage, on both the ankles and both the knees.
And the length of each of them by the quantity of four fingers, and the leg of the patient extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient is suspended over the head of the pole, and that he is in the middle; and the length of him from the ground the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong young man to press the head of the groin, and that he is suspended with the patient another young man, and the other young man presses the groin with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is in the outer part, then the patient must lie on his back as we have recommended in the having the groin; and be tightened the bandage on the leg of the patient properly and on the feet of him.
And then two poles are placed, one at the feet of him, and the other at the head; and then poles adjusted in the middle of the groin are placed on which wet cloths are enveloped, so that the patient is not damaged because the pole is between the thighs of him, because of which the extension to the inner parts is not pulled.
And then each minister extends to his part, and the doctor with his hand engulfs the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then the leg must be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narration about groin; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the groin of the patient, and then he pushes that with the other hand, and with that pushing he pauses that extended to the inner parts to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then take that the patient must not be extended to the inner parts, and is elevated on the ground; having take that he is placed on a hard cause, as if the swelling of him is dislocated in the outer parts, according to what we have said of the placing of him on two planks; and it is on his back and the bandages are extended, as we have said, there is no harm.
And take that compression be administered with a board on the place where the joint is.
Therefore, as the reduction is complicated according to what take — and the sign of the reduction of him is not hidden from you, and is that you extend from the patient two legs, and when you see those engulfs and the patient stretches his leg and expands that ses excusacio, then you know that the limb according to what take is returned — and then you adjust two things and pause plaster and extend with bandage so that the width is not bad in any of the parts; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or for four.
And then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg to the other.
Now, if you see those joints in alignment, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed, and therefore let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and bandage that, and extend that according to your first structure, and leave it for three days.
And then unwrap that, and make that one delay in walking for some days, until it is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the shoulder.
The shoulder is dislocated in three ways, which is dislocated to the outside parts, and to the inside parts, and to the parts forward to the parts backward; and to the front parts it is not dislocated.
And the sign of dislocation of it is that you command the patient to apply their leg to the groin; then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
And the restoration of all ways of it is that you make the patient straight, so that he already extends his leg, if he can on top of that; adhere your two to the face of it, and place his foot between your feet; and then make adhere the shoulder of it to your palms, and hold those between your fingers on the shoulder of it with strength, and another minister extends his leg while the shoulder returns to its place.
And the sign of the return of it is that his leg adheres to the groin with lightness without labor.
And then bandage that and make the leg adhere to the groin, and then tie those immediately with a bandage for three days or for four, and then unwrap those.
And do not use the patient for walking for some days, until it is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it to you is excused, then administer strong extension with ligaments of which in the cure of the hernia preceding narration.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle some times is sprained by small movement, and sometimes it is dislocated according to compliment, and the dislocation of it is made either to the inside parts or to the outside parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of the sprain of it is of lightness reduction, and it is that it be extended with ease with the hands and be aligned while it returns.
But in the cure of it when it is dislocated according to compliment, cover that you make the patient straight, and have that one minister strong and valiant of the part behind the back of it in the middle place of it.
And then you with your right hand the foot of the most sovereign part, and with your left hand of the part most below of the foot in the place of the ankle.
And then you pull the foot to you with your right hand, and then with the left for two times, and then extend with the left; and push the foot of the foot with the right towards the groin.
And do this two times according to that we have counted.
And then push the foot of the foot to the groin the third time, and you are at the ankle.
Therefore if it returns in one time or in two according to this manner, and if you see the foot aligned, it is good; and if not, then on it iterates the operation, for that returns.
But if to you is prohibited the reduction of it with that which we have recounted, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and stretch a padded cloth on the ground firmly, and that it lies between the thighs of it, and you have already enveloped on it cloth so that it does not burn the patient.
And then the minister of it holds its thighs, and another minister extends the leg with its hands or with ligaments that lie on the collar of the foot; and then extend that each minister against extension of its part, and the cloth be extending between the thighs of the patient retaining that, so that the body of it not be pulled to the parts below towards its extension.
And then the physician aligns the dislocation with its hands, and another minister holds the healthy thigh to the parts below, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore when the dislocation returns and is declared to you the health of the reduction of it, then pause bandage and extend that with ligaments firmly, and tie the foot with ligaments to the groin.
And cover that you fear or guard yourself of the nerves which are behind on the ankle, so that the ligament on that not be vehement and injure that firmly.
And then leave that for two days or for three; and if the ligament is softened, extend that.
And then unwrap that on the third day or on the fourth, and prohibit the patient from movement for 40 days. But if it is forced to go before that space, it is not made secure that the dislocation is not dissolved and corrupted on it, and then that which does not receive cure.
And if it happens to it abscess cut, then cover that you administer in the reduction of it that in the place where we preceded our narration, in another place of cure and of embrocation, until it is sprained.
Chapter .xxxiiii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the toes.
That the one that is dislocated be wrapped with little extension of his work; and that which is not difficult, raise it lightly.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the foot, then make the patient put his foot on a flat surface of the earth or on a table, and be standing thus as if he were going.
And then stand on his feet, and place your foot on that which is imminent from the joints, and then step on it with your foot with virtue until it returns, and that you see it already wrapped and does not appear in the eminence.
And then place under the foot of him a table that supports the whole foot, which has two ends, and then strengthen it with a decent and firm structure for three days; and then untie it and make him rest from walking for a few days, until it is strengthened and its return is assured.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the kinds of dislocations that are made with plague or with fracture or with any of them together.
When any of those causes happen, and you engage in the cure of it and in its restoration, many times the death occurs.
And for this, you should not presume over its cure, but rather the one who is wise in the art, of long usage, merciful and compassionate, tardy, not daring nor presumptuous; and that use in the beginning of medicines that cede abscesses, causing only so much, and leaving the patient to the divine judgment, but to the one whom you hope for salvation from perdition, with the alleviation of the illness, and in which you appear some hope. And therefore, strive as soon as possible to make its reduction at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes an abscess.
And therefore, if the limb returns as we want, then administer the regimen which causes a cauterized abscess and cures the plague with what you deem appropriate from the drying herbs.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and happens in the bones separated, then you pierce the stem of them, and do in that what we have said in the simple illnesses of this which is preceded in the places of those.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the entrance in the ways of fear according to what has preceded you my preaching; for this is better you owe your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more greetings to your true body those in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasim perfectly of which may God be the benefit. Amen.
